  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_39761472-6286-4ffd-9b66-eabd77894410'
      });

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_224496850" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/224496850" />
    <a title="Jo" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2467655-jo"><img alt="Jo" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596119990p2/2467655.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/224496850?book_show_action=true">Oct 17, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jo" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jo" href="/user/show/2467655-jo">Jo</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Jo by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/5535721-amy-turn-the-page">Amy (Turn the Page)</a>
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer224496850" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10341859755990840703"><b>
  <i> “How could ten forgettable minutes of not much turn both our lives inside out and upside down like this?”</i>
</b><br/><br/><b>Initial Final Page Thoughts.</b><br/>Yay!<br/><br/><b>High Points.</b><br/>Dante. Adam and his story. Let’s hear it for the boys! Daddies. Different perspectives. Acceptance. Sleepless nights. Walks in the park. Taking responsibilities. Hiccupping hearts. <i>”Just bringing up wind.”</i>. Living life out loud. <br/><br/><b>Low Points.</b><br/>I would really have loved to see more of the social implications that Dante faced. What <i>was</i> covered- his f</span>
  <span id="freeText10341859755990840703" style="display:none"><b>
  <i> “How could ten forgettable minutes of not much turn both our lives inside out and upside down like this?”</i>
</b><br /><br /><b>Initial Final Page Thoughts.</b><br />Yay!<br /><br /><b>High Points.</b><br />Dante. Adam and his story. Let’s hear it for the boys! Daddies. Different perspectives. Acceptance. Sleepless nights. Walks in the park. Taking responsibilities. Hiccupping hearts. <i>”Just bringing up wind.”</i>. Living life out loud. <br /><br /><b>Low Points.</b><br />I would really have loved to see more of the social implications that Dante faced. What <i>was</i> covered- his friend’s reactions, the social workers, the exchange in the doctor’s surgery- was done incredibly well but I just think having a bit more of an insight into those side of things would have made it even more realistic than it was. Sometimes I felt like these things were overlooked for the bigger picture and eclipsed by other issues that feature later on in the story. <br /><br /><b>Completely Unrelated Point</b><br />Re: The Cover. <br />I didn’t know Bruce Springsteen was starting his own baby-sitting club…. <br /><br /><b>Hero.</b><br />First up, I just have to say how refreshing it was to read a book about teenage pregnancy from the boy’s perspective. Whether it’s on TV, in books or even in the news, people assume that the dads always do a runner. I’m not saying it <i>doesn’t</i> happen, but it doesn’t <i>always</i> happen. The dads who stick around never seemed to get mentioned and they’re all painted with the same brush, so it was really great to read a story from a perspective that gets ignored too often. <br />Anyway…<br />Oh Dante, you poor little lamb. <br />If <i>I</i> had found out that I’d had a child on the day I got my A-Level results (not <i>entirely</i> sure how that would’ve worked) I would have been a jibbering wreck. And you should’ve seen me sans unexpected child; it wasn’t pretty, I assure you.<br />What I loved most about the character of Dante was that he went through a true learning curve and not just in how to look after an eleven month year old girl. This book is a crash course in learning to be selfless, learning to be compassionate, learning that life doesn’t always go as planned and learning to take responsibility for your own actions.<br />In the hands of another author, this story could have stumbled blindly into the preachy trap, but it never did. I never felt like I was being lectured or that she was making a political statement about the levels of teenage pregnancy in modern day Britain. <br />It’s always so satisfying to see a character start off a bit unlikeable and completely useless and watch as they mature and learn from their mistakes with little to no whinging. I hate characters who start off perfect and end just as perfect, where’s the fun in that? <br />Also, Dante’s a brilliant big brother. But I’m not saying anything else about <i>that</i> for fear I will cry The Ugly Cry. <br /><br /><b>Family.</b><br />I have so much love for this lot. I love how they interacted because it felt natural and funny without being forced. They had their problems and their skeletons in their closets but you could tell that they all really cared for each other and it was really special to read. <br />Also, Aunt Jackie was a firecracker. <br /><br /><b>Theme Tune.</b><br />….<br />I have to do it and I’m not even sorry.<br />If a song mentions the author then it’s pretty much a given it’s going to be the theme tune, right?<br /><br /><a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=YgFyi74DVjc&amp;ob=av2e" rel="nofollow noopener">Written in the Stars by Tinie Tempah ft Eric Turner. </a><br /><br />Also, I may or may not have developed some kind of knee-jerk reaction that whenever I say ‘Malorie Blackman’ out loud, I have to follow it immediately with “Where the hell’s all the sanity at? Damn.”<br /><br />Because I’m a ridiculous person. <br />But anyway, I don’t care because I have a lot of time for Mr Tempah and his crazy sun protectors. <br /><br />Also, this one is for Adam. <br /><br /><a href="http://www.youtube.com/watch?v=usksH8B07do" rel="nofollow noopener">Survive It by Ghostpoet. </a><br /><br /><i>I know<br />Times are hard<br />You’re against the wall, and<br />Your head is down, but<br />I think<br />You’ll get there soon<br />Just have a little faith, mate<br />It’ll turn out great.</i><br /><br /><b>Sadness Scale.</b><br />9/10.<br />Gosh. <br />I think a lot of people will be put off from reading this book because it seems like it could either be an “Issue Book” or full of LOLZ and so many hilarious stories that Selleck, Danson and Guttenburg will be hammering on your door so they can get in on the action. <br />And even though it <i>does</i> deal with issues and it is full of LOLZ and hilarious stories, it’s so much deeper than that. <br />I can’t tell you the reason why this book is getting such a high sadness scale (spoilers and all) but I had a lump in my throat for most of the second half. *sob*<br />In this book, Ms Blackman has created a story that is just as much about being male in modern day Britain, what it takes to be masculine, taking responsibility for your actions, family and, above all, staying true to yourself. <br /><br /><b>Recommended For.</b><br />People are looking for a book that offers a different and fascinating perspective on an, arguably, overdone subject. People are looking for a book where the boys take over. People who are reluctant to take their Beyonce posters down. People who want to live their life out loud. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="10341859755990840703" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1491042-aly-fantasy4eva?shelf=uk-bookish-love">uk-bookish-love</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer221853498" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8911917228954353421">After reading NOUGHTS AND CROSSES, the author quickly became one for me to look out for. So I was thrilled to receive this book.<br/><br/>It was freaking awesome.<br/><br/>Just imagine. A 17 year old boy (Dante) that has his life all planned out. He is just about to receive his A Level results and is well on his way to University to study Journalism. But then your ex girlfriend pops up. She has a baby. She claims this baby is your baby. Goes out for a few minutes, promising to return. She never comes back.<br/><br/>I was a l</span>
  <span id="freeText8911917228954353421" style="display:none">After reading NOUGHTS AND CROSSES, the author quickly became one for me to look out for. So I was thrilled to receive this book.<br /><br />It was freaking awesome.<br /><br />Just imagine. A 17 year old boy (Dante) that has his life all planned out. He is just about to receive his A Level results and is well on his way to University to study Journalism. But then your ex girlfriend pops up. She has a baby. She claims this baby is your baby. Goes out for a few minutes, promising to return. She never comes back.<br /><br />I was a little gob-smacked when it happened. Of course the synopsis gives it away but it's baffling nevertheless. <br /><br />In the midst of all this there are family problems. His mother passed away and his father has never quite been the same. He doesn't have much of a relationship with either of his sons and struggles to show emotion - which really pulls a strain on the whole family dynamic. You then have his brother Adam. A young boy that has to live with his family denial every day. With his family turning a blind eye to what he is, and by him getting bullied by his big brother, Dante' friends, things are really taking their toll on him. <br /><br />You then have Dante, this boy, who struggles with the anger, guilt and confusion of a baby he never wanted or planned. You watch on as the anger resides and he starts to worry about how he can be a better father. This transition is remarkable. I also really loved how his father was a little harsh towards him. He didn't sugar-coat things. He let him have it. He explained that this child is his, therefore his responsibility and making sacrifices are one of the many things that he will have to do.<br /><br />It's a thought - provoking read,a learning experience and an eye opener - quite inspiring also. BOY'S DON'T CRY is definitely a novel that all of us can learn from.</span>

          </span>

<div id="review_240151978" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/240151978" />
    <a title="Anna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5744962-anna"><img alt="Anna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1363829197p2/5744962.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/240151978?book_show_action=true">Nov 27, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Anna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Anna" href="/user/show/5744962-anna">Anna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5744962-anna?shelf=ebooks">ebooks</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5744962-anna?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5744962-anna?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5744962-anna?shelf=inspirational">inspirational</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer240151978" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17126497383251737738">What an amazing read! I still can't keep my mind out of it. <br/><br/>I am still bemused that as much as I want to tell the tale I'll try to keep it super vague and not let you miss all the action. <br/><br/>BOYS DON'T CRY is, as the last time I checked, the first book I read about young, single parent/s. Dante is just your 17-year-old dreamer, achiever, and typical teenager who parties and went out with friends. But then in the middle of the biggest moment he's been waiting to turn up in life, he was startled to k</span>
  <span id="freeText17126497383251737738" style="display:none">What an amazing read! I still can't keep my mind out of it. <br /><br />I am still bemused that as much as I want to tell the tale I'll try to keep it super vague and not let you miss all the action. <br /><br />BOYS DON'T CRY is, as the last time I checked, the first book I read about young, single parent/s. Dante is just your 17-year-old dreamer, achiever, and typical teenager who parties and went out with friends. But then in the middle of the biggest moment he's been waiting to turn up in life, he was startled to know that he became a father. And so, the story go on...<br /><br />As I read, I was so sad and so happy at the same time. The characters, especially Dante, Adam, Dad, Aunt Jackie, and Emma are so very likable. I love how everyone of them are flawed in their own way and that whatever they want to do or what they want to be, they held to that with pride. I realized it is never easy raising a child but, heck even thinking of taking care of one is hard. But life's little surprises makes it more fun and exciting that it'll be part of your life that you don't want to be apart from it. That was what this book has taught me. Dante had never thought what has come his way with Emma, but he's was trying his best to cope, care and nurture her and really let her feel that she's his own. It's actually made me tear everytime he regards Emma as "my daughter", so cute! Just when you thought a family will never have a chance on a harmonious, happy life since the loss of someone very special, think of it as "You lose some, you win some." And on Dante's case, they lost their mother over her death but they were rewarded with a very lovely, beautiful angel that turns their life upside down and makes their world go round. <br /><br />The characters relationship to one another is very touching. How they are very concerned with one another it make me cry every time. I wish my big brother would be like that! I just realized that Emma is the only female in the household full of men. And I mean men in physicality, if you know what I mean. ;) She's the rose among the thorns, the moon among the stars, the light in the dark (yeah, I should probably stop) :)<br /><br />I really liked this one, scratch that, I love this one. I love how real and relatable they are that proves us nobody is perfect. I especially love the ending and that's how ending should be. They left you with your imagination to go wild and wild as want it to be. The verdict is it'll turn out great in the latter.<br /><br />P.S. I thought I won't like that much but ended up loving it. ☺<br /><br /><br />xx<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17126497383251737738" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_223173459" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/223173459" />
    <a title="Alice Radwell" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3039193-alice-radwell"><img alt="Alice Radwell" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1530611835p2/3039193.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/223173459?book_show_action=true">Oct 14, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Alice Radwell" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Alice Radwell" href="/user/show/3039193-alice-radwell">Alice Radwell</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780552548625" href="/book/show/9607981-boys-don-t-cry">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3039193-alice-radwell?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3039193-alice-radwell?shelf=socially-challenging">socially-challenging</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3039193-alice-radwell?shelf=own-it">own-it</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3039193-alice-radwell?shelf=diverse-voices">diverse-voices</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer223173459" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11716263217940791376">This novel caught my interest because I thought I was in for a unique read on the overworked teenage-parent theme, and in some ways I wasn't wrong. Emotionally charged and morally engaging, the story takes us on a journey not only of unexpected fatherhood, but of the whirlwind of family in all it's colours and shapes. Dante, our protagonist, takes to sudden parenthood like a bird to the ocean, and his initial reactions and reasonings are easy to relate to. It was a nice change to witness the fat</span>
  <span id="freeText11716263217940791376" style="display:none">This novel caught my interest because I thought I was in for a unique read on the overworked teenage-parent theme, and in some ways I wasn't wrong. Emotionally charged and morally engaging, the story takes us on a journey not only of unexpected fatherhood, but of the whirlwind of family in all it's colours and shapes. Dante, our protagonist, takes to sudden parenthood like a bird to the ocean, and his initial reactions and reasonings are easy to relate to. It was a nice change to witness the father being left to tackle the struggles of an infant rather than the mother, and to follow the process of having to learn to interact with, and even love, a child from the perspective of a young-man.<br /><br />Blackman uses a duel-first-person format, switching between the point of view of new-father Dante, and his eccentric brother Adam, flipping frequently (at first) to give a coin toss view of a family dynamic. In my opinion, Blackman either needed to remove Adam's chapters completely, or go the whole-hog and include his story in its entirety; as it is she does neither, and we're left with surplus chapters that really add no depth, and act solely to encourage pity. It would have been interesting if the sole focus had been on Emma; I didn't really feel past the first few chapters after her introduction there was much tension surrounding her, and Adam's story (which slowly takes precedence) was ridiculously over-exaggerated, and hard-to-believe. Even Adam himself is presented as a stereotypical homosexual, which made his plight even less interesting to follow. There are some genuine moments of brotherly bonding between the two sides, and the different view-points with regards to their father were engaging.<br /><br />I didn't make much of the writing. During the beginning, the book is very preachy, using dialogue between father and son to determine an understanding that a child changes a life forever, but the whole interaction was cliché and over-stated. There are several cringe worthy examples of this before the novel loses it's focus and drives into other teen-issues with no time to look back. The dialogue is clumsy, unfunny where it means to add humour, and often unrealistic. By the end some chapters read like an information pamphlet on homophobia and suicide, and the initial baby problems are stuffed in willy-nilly almost just to remind us Emma is still an important aspect of the story.<br /><br />I wanted to enjoy this book far more than I did; I think I would have been better engaged reading more about a boy struggling to get-to-grips with being a father, and perhaps some of the social and legal aspects of the issue. This would probably be a good read for younger-teens, to gain a better understanding of responsibility and childcare, while also introducing other social issues that come with growing-up.<br /><br />Shamless plug time :). For more reviews please visit <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="http://alicereadsbooks.wordpress.com/">http://alicereadsbooks.wordpress.com/</a> and let me know what you thought. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11716263217940791376" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1991607286" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1991607286" />
    <a title="Liam Owens" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13746721-liam-owens"><img alt="Liam Owens" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1516213300p2/13746721.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1991607286?book_show_action=true">May 05, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Liam Owens" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Liam Owens" href="/user/show/13746721-liam-owens">Liam Owens</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13746721-liam-owens?shelf=masculinity">masculinity</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/13746721-liam-owens?shelf=lgbt">lgbt</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1991607286" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7217115891012756426">I read this book as I'm researching how modern masculinity is represented in contemporary teen fiction as part of my Masters degree in Children's Literature. This is the first book I've read by Malorie Blackman, though I had pretty high expectations as I know she's a celebrated author who has received considerable recognition for her contributions to children's fiction.<br/><br/>In short, I thought this was a fantastic book. Teenage pregnancy isn't exactly a new topic in YA, but the decision to approach t</span>
  <span id="freeText7217115891012756426" style="display:none">I read this book as I'm researching how modern masculinity is represented in contemporary teen fiction as part of my Masters degree in Children's Literature. This is the first book I've read by Malorie Blackman, though I had pretty high expectations as I know she's a celebrated author who has received considerable recognition for her contributions to children's fiction.<br /><br />In short, I thought this was a fantastic book. Teenage pregnancy isn't exactly a new topic in YA, but the decision to approach this issue from a male perspective was, I thought, refreshing. When we think of teen fathers, we automatically assume their parenting is inadequate and that they aren't able to raise a child as well as a mother. The book plays on these stereotypes - as well as other assumptions surrounding masculinity - and does an excellent job exposing just how superficial and unfounded they are. It also raises some interesting questions about a father's rights, some of which I felt could have been explored in more depth (for example, the scene towards the end of the novel where Dante reveals he's terrified that Melanie might come back and take Emma away from him and how that won't be a problem because she's her mother).<br /><br />Whilst we follow Dante as he comes to terms with fatherhood, we also see life through the eyes of his younger brother, Adam, who is openly gay but has a difficult time trying to convince his family and friends to accept his sexuality. I found Adam's narrative to be just as interesting as Dante's, and I thought the two contrasting storylines did a good job of interrogating what it means to be male from two different perspectives. Adam's storyline takes a considerably darker turn towards the end of the novel (which I won't go into because of spoilers), but I felt it did an excellent job of exploring the inner-struggle of teenage boys who don't seem to conform to conventional standards of masculinity. <br /><br />My only criticism is that the writing was a little simplistic at times, which makes me think this book probably leans towards the younger end of the YA spectrum. I also found some of the plot twists a little predictable, but this is probably because I've read quite a few novels that centre on gay and/or non-stereotypically male protagonists.<br /><br />Favourite line: "Boys don't cry, but real men do."</span>
  <a data-text-id="7217115891012756426" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review1991607286">

  <span id="freeText7338414875361177322" style="display:none"><br />It was sweet book, much better than the other book I had read by Malorie Blackman which I had some issues with. <br />I liked that we had Adam's POV and his story because otherwise the book would have been much groomier. <div id="review_172753298" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/172753298" />
    <a title="Amy (Turn the Page)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5535721-amy-turn-the-page"><img alt="Amy (Turn the Page)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1343817781p2/5535721.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/172753298?book_show_action=true">Jun 01, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Amy (Turn the Page)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Amy (Turn the Page)" href="/user/show/5535721-amy-turn-the-page">Amy (Turn the Page)</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5535721-amy-turn-the-page?shelf=my-favorite-books">my-favorite-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer172753298" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14097008408306970094">I am a huge fan of Malorie Blackman and advocator of her work ever since I first read Noughts and Crosses many years ago. Not only does Blackman create extremely well written books with relatable characters, but she also tackles difficult, sometimes controversial, subjects for YA literature, in an honest and thought provoking way. Boy’s Don’t Cry is a powerful and unexpected novel. I couldn’t put it down and ended up reading through until 5 in the morning, and then I couldn’t sleep for thinking </span>
  <span id="freeText14097008408306970094" style="display:none">I am a huge fan of Malorie Blackman and advocator of her work ever since I first read Noughts and Crosses many years ago. Not only does Blackman create extremely well written books with relatable characters, but she also tackles difficult, sometimes controversial, subjects for YA literature, in an honest and thought provoking way. Boy’s Don’t Cry is a powerful and unexpected novel. I couldn’t put it down and ended up reading through until 5 in the morning, and then I couldn’t sleep for thinking about it.<br /><br />Boys Don’t Cry is told from two points of view, Dantes’ and Adams’ – to begin with the reason for this is unclear and though I liked Adam’s upbeat narrative in contrast to Dante’s, I couldn’t figure out why we were switching between the two. This lasted for about 70 pages and from then on I was hooked. I hadn’t realised when I started reading Boys Don’t Cry that this was a book about much, much, more than just teenage parenthood – Blackman has blended together two stories that deal with two very different topics. I’m not going to spoil it for anyone by telling you everything this one book manages to cover, and cover well. I will say that though, that while I loved both stories, it was Adam I grew particularly attached to as a character, and it was his voice and his journey that struck me the hardest and stayed with me long after I had finished reading it.<br /><br />Boys Don’t Cry takes a unique look at being a teenage parent, as it is told from the boys point of view. I have to applaud Blackman for choosing to do this as it is a topic that young male readers should be able to read about and because too often (in life and in books) the father is either forgotten, or considered less important in a child’s life. Then there is the stereotype that if anyone will walk away, it will be the father. In typical fashion, Blackman disputes all these assumptions (and more) in Boy’s Con’t Cry. She is an author you can depend upon to challenge the every day stereotypes all around us – her writing makes you think and I love her for it.<br /><br />A few scenes that stood out for me were when Dante takes his daughter out for the first time and has to deal with strangers’ prejudices and judgement once they learn Emma is his. I see and hear people dismissing young parents all the time, always assuming they are lazy and exploiting the benefit system. By experiencing this type of public reaction with Dante, Blackman really hits home just how hurtful and wrong it is to judge people without knowing them – reminding us that just because someone is a young parent – doesn’t mean they are a bad one.<br /><br />Though Boys Don’t Cry focuses on the prejudices young dads receive in our society, here Blackman briefly touches on how young mothers are looked down on and treated by society as well – you can almost see the word ‘slut’ forming in this woman’s mind as she looks at Collette. It is a sharp reminder not to judge Melanie too harshly for running out (another stereotype – it is considered pretty normal for the father to leave, but unforgivable if the mother does), as this is doubtless what she would of dealt over and over again, on her alone, for the past 18 months.<br /><br />Blackman also writes some fantastic scenes surrounding the local social worker. We immediately feel as defensive towards her as Dante does and assume she is not only a busybody poking her nose in but also that she is out to trick Dante and wants to take Emma away from him. Why do automatically feel this way? It seems not a day can go by without the papers reporting something derogatory about social workers. I loved that as we, and Dante, got to know her a little better, we see that this woman only wants the best for Emma and is, in reality, there to help Dante keep his daughter. It was great, for once, to see a social worker portrayed as someone who cares, who has a difficult, at times impossible, job, and who has to deal with an unfair reputation. I think society in general forgets what an incredibly hard and incredibly amazing job these people can, and do, do. Once again we have another character who, in their own way, has to fight against society’s opinion of them, a character who has been judged unfairly; prompting the reader to confront their own reactions and their own prejudices as they read.<br /><br />Watching Dante fall in love with his daughter is heartwarming without being overly sentimental – the tone in this book is just right to appeal to young male and female readers. What’s so great about Boys Don’t Cry is that it isn’t a cute story. It deals with the harsh reality of life as a teenage parent and doesn’t shy away from difficult and, at times, shocking truths. Dante doesn’t love his daughter immediately, or choose her above his own desires – it is hard, he has to make many sacrifices and deal with a lot of pressure from everyone around him. He comes close to breaking down and almost hits Emma – a feeling/moment I’m sure a lot of stressed out parents can relate to, but one that no one really talks about. We come to understand that coming close to snapping doesn’t make Dante a bad person, but walking away from the situation and asking for help does make him a good father. Throughout there is a lovely family dynamic and I enjoyed reading how certain events bring this family closer together and how they come out all the more stronger for it.<br /><br />If Dante’s story is touching, Adam’s story is hard-hitting and heartbreaking. Already a strong piece of fiction, this is what made Boys Don’t Cry unforgetable for me. Both Adam and Dante are obstricized by society but for very different reasons and watching them deal with that, and reading about what happens them, is both upsetting and uplifting – particularly in Adam’s case. I loved his character from the start.<br /><br />*SPOILER*<br /><br /><br /><br />It was refreshing to read about a character who is unashamed of his sexuality and Blackman makes a vitally important point that some younger readers may not yet understand: that Adam doesn’t choose to be gay, it is who he is and more importantly, he is happy with who he is. <br /><br />*END OF SPOILER*<br /><br /><br /><br />What made this novel so brilliant for me were the characters. All of them, even the secondary characters, felt very real – we can identify with them. Reading this story and experiencing things from a different point of view makes us question our own opinions and prejudices. Boys Don’t Cry tackles many important subjects. It shows us the importance of understanding, compassion and acceptance – and the power of words. The courage to be yourself and to confront ignorance and hatred in all its forms. A stunning book that every one should pick up, regardless of age or gender – because I think you will learn something about yourself and others by the end of it. Beautiful story, beautiful characters and beautiful narration. And yes – it made me cry.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14097008408306970094" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_119888558" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/119888558" />
    <a title="Sarah" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3344685-sarah"><img alt="Sarah" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1388694025p2/3344685.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/119888558?book_show_action=true">Sep 03, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sarah" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sarah" href="/user/show/3344685-sarah">Sarah</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3344685-sarah?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3344685-sarah?shelf=read-in-2010">read-in-2010</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3344685-sarah?shelf=for-review-read">for-review-read</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3344685-sarah?shelf=books-i-own-print">books-i-own-print</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3344685-sarah?shelf=signed">signed</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3344685-sarah?shelf=british-author">british-author</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer119888558" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13858514210932943638">Dante is just seventeen years old and is anxiously waiting for his A level results to see if he can get a place at university to study journalism. He is an intelligent young man - proven by the fact that he has taken his A levels a year early - and he is looking forward to a glittering future. Everything changes the day his results arrive, but not in the way he was expecting. When his ex-girlfriend turns up out of the blue with a pushchair he is shocked and horrified - especially when she tells </span>
  <span id="freeText13858514210932943638" style="display:none">Dante is just seventeen years old and is anxiously waiting for his A level results to see if he can get a place at university to study journalism. He is an intelligent young man - proven by the fact that he has taken his A levels a year early - and he is looking forward to a glittering future. Everything changes the day his results arrive, but not in the way he was expecting. When his ex-girlfriend turns up out of the blue with a pushchair he is shocked and horrified - especially when she tells him the baby is his. Melanie asks him to look after baby Emma while she does some shopping and disappears before even giving him time to reply. When she calls him to say she won't be coming back Dante finds his life has been turned upside down. Although he scored top marks in his exams it looks like university is no longer an option - he is a single father now and his daughter needs him.<br /><br />Boys Don't Cry is the first book I've read by Malorie Blackman but it definitely won't be the last. I've heard such good things about her Noughts and Crosses series and after reading this book I can't wait to get stuck into those books too. Boys Don't Cry is an amazing read and one I would recommend to anyone - adults and teenagers alike - it will turn everything you've ever thought about teenage parents on it's head. This book made me laugh a lot and cry a little - it really was a roller coaster of emotion and one I couldn't put down - I read it in just one sitting.<br /><br />Dante is a great character - he is smart and ambitious, the last person you would expect to become a teenage father but that just goes to show how easy it is to get into a situation like this. After just one drunken, very forgettable sexual encounter at a party Dante now finds himself a single father - something he definitely wasn't planning and isn't at all prepared for. The emotions he goes through are so realistic that this book could easily be a personal account of a true story. At first he is shocked and then comes anger - he didn't ask for this &amp; it shouldn't have happened to him - but his feelings for Emma gradually turn into love and he comes through for her in a big way. Yes he makes mistakes along the way but that just makes him more real in my eyes, I don't think anyone can say they've never done something they regretted in life. Human nature is flawed and it's how we cope with the challenges along the way that shows what kind of person we really are and as Dante becomes a man he becomes the kind of person anyone would be proud to know.<br /><br />The story shows just how hard it is to be a parent at any age but it must be particularly difficult when you still feel like a child yourself. Dante is lucky to have the support of his father and his younger brother, both of whom throw themselves into helping him care for Emma. Although Dante's father is disappointed with his son he is an instant support and dotes on Emma from the beginning. Even with the support of his family Dante finds out just how difficult it is caring for a baby and gradually comes to realise how hard it must have been for Melanie who was completely on her own. Dante also has to face prejudice wherever he turns - he can't even go to the shops without people criticising him for being such a young father and he is treated even worse because of the fact that he is a single father.<br /><br />Although I loved Dante I think it was Adam that stole my heart most - he is such a sweet character with a sunny attitude that you just can't help falling for. Adam is openly gay and proud to tell the world about it - a character to admire for sure and the challenges he faces along the way really were heartbreaking to read about. I just wanted to climb into the book and protect him (I'd have given Emma a massive hug while I was in there because she was just too adorable for words!). I was routing for Adam the whole way through and just wanted him to be happy.<br /><br />Boys Don't Cry in no way glamorises teenage pregnancy, quite the opposite in fact as it shows just how hard parenting can be. Although it also shows the joys of parenthood I know this book would have made me extra careful as a teenager because I wouldn't have wanted to end up in Dante's situation. I really think this book is a must read for all teenagers and their parents - it would be a great way to start a discussion about using protection and being careful. I love the way the story shows that it isn't just up to the girl to take responsibility for using protection. This coming of age story may be written from the male perspective but it is relevant for both boys and girls and I think anyone would find something they can relate to in the characters. I found this was a heartwarming read that really shouldn't be missed and I'd strongly recommend you order yourself a copy of this book now.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13858514210932943638" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1759499835" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1759499835" />
    <a title="James" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41609617-james"><img alt="James" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1539156508p2/41609617.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1759499835?book_show_action=true">Sep 19, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="James" class="user" itemprop="url" name="James" href="/user/show/41609617-james">James</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1759499835" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16266359264964128012">Whilst primarily a novel aimed squarely at the 'young adult' market - as with all good books, this transcends the confines and limitations of that genre.<br/><br/>Ostensibly a simple, straightforward and unsurprising coming of age story of contemporary family life and dynamics - this is powerfully, emotionally and movingly told.<br/><br/>The standard of writing and the quality of characterisation ensure that this story avoids becoming what could so easily have been a two dimensional, patronising and stereotypical t</span>
  <span id="freeText16266359264964128012" style="display:none">Whilst primarily a novel aimed squarely at the 'young adult' market - as with all good books, this transcends the confines and limitations of that genre.<br /><br />Ostensibly a simple, straightforward and unsurprising coming of age story of contemporary family life and dynamics - this is powerfully, emotionally and movingly told.<br /><br />The standard of writing and the quality of characterisation ensure that this story avoids becoming what could so easily have been a two dimensional, patronising and stereotypical tale of teenage 'life by numbers' - resulting in what would have been an insubstantial and forgettable read. <br /><br />Malorie Blackman tackles important issues here and does so with skill, respect and without passing judgement. This is a very human story about growing up fast - which comes highly recommended. </span>
  <a data-text-id="16266359264964128012" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">

<div id="review_656116142" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/656116142" />
    <a title="Tara" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/18857079-tara"><img alt="Tara" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1364647435p2/18857079.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/656116142?book_show_action=true">Jul 01, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tara" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tara" href="/user/show/18857079-tara">Tara</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer656116142" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11822694060851158841">*****SPOILERS*****<br/>I really don't get what Blackman was getting at with this book. She starts well, illustrating the issues faced by Dante as he's thrown into the world of being a single teenage dad and showing how he learns to cope with it... but that's it as far as that part of the book goes.<br/><br/>The latter third or so if the book is preoccupied with his brother Adam and homophobia. Did Blackman start writing one book and then decide halfway through she was out of ideas so let's just entirely change</span>
  <span id="freeText11822694060851158841" style="display:none">*****SPOILERS*****<br />I really don't get what Blackman was getting at with this book. She starts well, illustrating the issues faced by Dante as he's thrown into the world of being a single teenage dad and showing how he learns to cope with it... but that's it as far as that part of the book goes.<br /><br />The latter third or so if the book is preoccupied with his brother Adam and homophobia. Did Blackman start writing one book and then decide halfway through she was out of ideas so let's just entirely change the central plotline? <br /><br />And what happens to Melanie? We don't hear from her at all after she's dumped baby Emma. I'm sure there's a LOT more to her story than we're given in this novel. My worldview tells me it takes a lot for a mother to abandon her child, her child who she decided to keep despite her family's wishes - to the point she's kicked out.<br /><br />I was hoping to see a story where the single father prevails and is able to build a stable career over time and support his daughter despite all the hurdles. I wanted to see Blackman telling the story of how becoming a young single parent doesn't mean the end of your aspired life. The story just comes to an abrupt and unsatisfactory ending - sure, the family are all happy and united... but they were anyway before Adam gets beaten up by his ex-partner. That's why I say it's as if the homophobia thing was just crowbarred in as filler and to provide an excuse for a conclusion.<br /><br />I made a note while reading that I didn't realise until towards the end of the book the family were black - which says a lot about the description that goes into the characters. I realised then I have no idea what any of the characters look like (all I knew formerly was that Emmahad black hair and Dante looked similar to her).<br /><br /><br />So yeah, in all, unimpressed and disappointed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11822694060851158841" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1740526366" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer11148191297466424136">This is excellent. As you would expect from Malorie Blackman: a pacy, intelligent, emotionally-charged, contemporary coming-of-age story, which focuses on young and unwanted fatherhood but also deals with issues around sexuality, bereavement, family and relationships. Maybe it's a tad predictable - but even while Blackman is presenting harsh realities she is sensitive to young readers' needs, and the sentimentality is woven with a couple of loose threads left for tying up later. Indeed, there is</span>
  <span id="freeText11148191297466424136" style="display:none">This is excellent. As you would expect from Malorie Blackman: a pacy, intelligent, emotionally-charged, contemporary coming-of-age story, which focuses on young and unwanted fatherhood but also deals with issues around sexuality, bereavement, family and relationships. Maybe it's a tad predictable - but even while Blackman is presenting harsh realities she is sensitive to young readers' needs, and the sentimentality is woven with a couple of loose threads left for tying up later. Indeed, there is noise about a second novel (Heartbreak Girl) in this series, due this year, but even Blackman's own website (at the time of writing) fails to provide firm info about this. </span>
  <a data-text-id="11148191297466424136" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_124310438" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/124310438" />
    <a title="Dwayne" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3273496-dwayne"><img alt="Dwayne" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1266450721p2/3273496.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/124310438?book_show_action=true">Oct 01, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dwayne" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dwayne" href="/user/show/3273496-dwayne">Dwayne</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer124310438" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer41093665811532502">Reviewed @ <a href='http://www.withoutabookshelf.blogspot.com' rel='nofollow noopener'>Girls Without a Bookshelf</a>.<br/><br/>There is no one word that can describe just how good of a read Boys Don't Cry is. This book is one of the few that should be read by every teenage boy in the world - it's packed with life's lessons and I'm sure I'm not the only one who says that this book is a thought-provoking and a reflective read.<br/><br/>When Dante Bridgeman finds the biggest surprise in his life lying in a baby buggy, to say that his life is turned upside down is an understatement. Suddenly, he i</span>
  <span id="freeText41093665811532502" style="display:none">Reviewed @ <a href="http://www.withoutabookshelf.blogspot.com" rel="nofollow noopener">Girls Without a Bookshelf</a>.<br /><br />There is no one word that can describe just how good of a read Boys Don't Cry is. This book is one of the few that should be read by every teenage boy in the world - it's packed with life's lessons and I'm sure I'm not the only one who says that this book is a thought-provoking and a reflective read.<br /><br />When Dante Bridgeman finds the biggest surprise in his life lying in a baby buggy, to say that his life is turned upside down is an understatement. Suddenly, he is no longer a typical student waiting for his A level results and university, but a single father to an eleven-month baby, Emma. With his ex-girlfriend and the baby's mother Melanie suddenly MIA, he finds his future veering dangerously towards the unknown. The changes in Dante's life - every minute details - is well described and highlighted. I'm familiar with the difficulty of babysitting, but not quite with the difficulty of single parenting. Dante's narration is vivid and so real it's almost like a first hand account. Nothing quite gets to you than the voice of someone who has really been there - I got that from Dante. His sacrifices opens the reader's eyes to the reality of being a parent - single, teenage or otherwise. His concerns starts from the basic (what about university? How do I stop her from crying?) and evolves to the bigger (will I be a good father? How will I provide for Emma?) questions. I am a lot like Dante in situation (A level results, university...) but it is his questions that makes him so normal, so average. That, more than anything, makes Dante real. His actions, his thoughts and his words show his flaws, but it also channels his growth as a person, as a brother, as a son and as a father to Emma. <br /><br />Interspersed with Dante's life changing moments are his brother Adam's. My heart went out to Adam in this book - he's a cheerful lad who is neither ashamed nor afraid of his sexuality. The challenges that face him are not the easiest ones to face nor read. Despite that, he has an indomitable spirit that rises up and faces these challenges head on. Of course, Adam has his share of challenges, but with his family's support never once leaving his side, Adam's strength is clear for all to see. I'm not sure who between him and Emma I find more adorable - but both of them are near top of the list of characters I love hugely.<br /><br />There are varying sub plots in the book in support of the main plot. These elaborates more on the characters and are very well ingrained with the narrative. Mostly, they are resolved and I am one happy reader with how everything turned out. The secondary characters are involved in these subplots and are also very important as they are foils to and frame the protagonists. Quite simply, I have no complaints! It's my first Malorie Blackman read and I sure will check out more of her works.<br /><br />Boys Don't Cry is an eye-opener, a complex novel that is one of the few which effectively thrusts the reader into issues that matter most in the big world. It made me cry, it made me think and it made me want to read so much more. I love this!<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="41093665811532502" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_123944348" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/123944348" />
    <a title="Jennifer Wardrip" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1125613-jennifer-wardrip"><img alt="Jennifer Wardrip" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442657515p2/1125613.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/123944348?book_show_action=true">Sep 29, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jennifer Wardrip" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jennifer Wardrip" href="/user/show/1125613-jennifer-wardrip">Jennifer Wardrip</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1125613-jennifer-wardrip?shelf=trt-gold-star-award-winner">trt-gold-star-award-winner</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1125613-jennifer-wardrip?shelf=trt-posted-reviews">trt-posted-reviews</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1125613-jennifer-wardrip?shelf=read-by-other-reviewers">read-by-other-reviewers</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer123944348" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5591037882476919488">BOYS DON'T CRY by Malorie Blackman is an emotionally charged story about love and family and the different shapes they sometimes take. This book is one you won't want to miss.<br/><br/>Dante is anxiously awaiting his A Level exam results. He is proud of himself for taking the exams a year early, and he's eager to know if his scores will have him headed to university in the near future. But when a knock at the door brings his old girlfriend and not the postman, Dante is in for the surprise of his life.<br/><br/>Mela</span>
  <span id="freeText5591037882476919488" style="display:none">BOYS DON'T CRY by Malorie Blackman is an emotionally charged story about love and family and the different shapes they sometimes take. This book is one you won't want to miss.<br /><br />Dante is anxiously awaiting his A Level exam results. He is proud of himself for taking the exams a year early, and he's eager to know if his scores will have him headed to university in the near future. But when a knock at the door brings his old girlfriend and not the postman, Dante is in for the surprise of his life.<br /><br />Melanie has been gone for over a year. The last time they spent any time together was at a friend's party. That night they were both drunk and ended up in an upstairs bedroom, where they had a less-than-memorable sexual encounter. Now, Melanie was sitting in his living room holding a baby on her lap. Dante is at first confused and then shocked when Melanie tells him the baby is his daughter, Emma.<br /><br />If finding out he has a daughter is not enough, Melanie asks Dante to watch the baby for a bit while she goes out to run an errand. Before he has a chance to object, she is gone. And not long after, Melanie calls from her mobile phone and tells Dante she won't be coming back.<br /><br />Dante's father is furious when he hears about his son's irresponsible sexual behavior, but to Dante's amazement, he immediately leaps into action, making a plan and getting the supplies they need to care for a baby. Dante is equally shocked when his younger brother, Adam, falls head-over-heels in love with little Emma. All Dante can think about is how to get Melanie to come back and get him out of this impossible situation.<br /><br />Even though his A Level exam scores are perfect, Dante knows his plans for university and the future have become a distant dream. He quickly learns being a father might be the hardest job on earth. The more time he spends with Emma, the more his love begins to blossom. With the help of his dad, his brother, and his aunt, Dante begins to take his responsibility seriously.<br /><br />Maybe it is learning to love his daughter or maybe it's spending more time at home, but Dante also begins to discover his usually cheerful younger brother has some problems of his own. He finds himself filling not only the role of new father, but also that of protective older brother. Through it all he learns the true meaning of love and all the joy and pain it can bring.<br /><br />Author Malorie Blackman takes the usual teen parenthood story and turns it upside-down. Readers will be quickly drawn into Dante's experiences and will feel every moment of fear, regret, and exhilaration as he deals with all life has thrown his way. There are fabulous moments of joy, flashes of anger, and terrible tragedies as this odd family struggles to hold it all together.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5591037882476919488" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2948987843" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2948987843" />
    <a title="Charlotte" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/86487438-charlotte"><img alt="Charlotte" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1596634952p2/86487438.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2948987843?book_show_action=true">Aug 24, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Charlotte" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Charlotte" href="/user/show/86487438-charlotte">Charlotte</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2948987843" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3673542142952163126">Wow. I have read a few of Malorie blackmans previous books but I had forgotten how great she really was. Boys don’t cry follows Dante, a 17 year old with dreams of university and a journalist career. He has his life mapped out and becoming a single father at 17 wasn’t part of the plan. <br/><br/>We see all the characters,Dante especially, transform as characters. While I expedited the Dante from the first few chapters to fail at fatherhood he excels much to my surprise. He sacrifices everything he knew fo</span>
  <span id="freeText3673542142952163126" style="display:none">Wow. I have read a few of Malorie blackmans previous books but I had forgotten how great she really was. Boys don’t cry follows Dante, a 17 year old with dreams of university and a journalist career. He has his life mapped out and becoming a single father at 17 wasn’t part of the plan. <br /><br />We see all the characters,Dante especially, transform as characters. While I expedited the Dante from the first few chapters to fail at fatherhood he excels much to my surprise. He sacrifices everything he knew for Emma which I admire considering as the lady in the book said “at least he stuck around and didn’t do a runner”. For me I have definitely re thought my view of teenage parents and I admire them for having the courage to be a parent despite what everyone says. I understand all situations are different and I’m sure the one of Dante and Emma is a bit idealistic but the message and creating an understanding of teenage parents, especially fathers, is what is important. <br /><br />It touches upon many other issues not just teenage fatherhood but homophobia, partnerships, life choices and family relationships. The courage Dante, Adam and their dad have and how they deal with all the situations is definitely something I will take with me. <br /><br />What makes his book unique is I have never come across a book about teenage fatherhood. The plot is slow and not all that interesting if I’m honest. I would’ve liked to see an epilogue of how Dante, Adam and Emma are doing and I feel that after reading this book I deserve that at the least.<br /><br />Read 2: <br />I loved it just as much a second time and believe I was affected yet again by this book and reminded of a world very different to my own. I feel a greater understanding and not only understanding but awe for the struggle and perseverance of single parents, parents in general! As well as the struggles other young people face in their life and the actions it can cause them to take (like Adams suicide attempt) all these characters are so well written and I love seeing Emma Develop into a sweet little toddler as well as Dante grow up tremendously and unfortunately seeing Adam lose some of his innocence. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="3673542142952163126" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_3211802110" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3211802110" />
    <a title="Paul Waters" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/110224081-paul-waters"><img alt="Paul Waters" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1581886376p2/110224081.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3211802110?book_show_action=true">Mar 01, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Paul Waters" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Paul Waters" href="/user/show/110224081-paul-waters">Paul Waters</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780552548625" href="/book/show/9607981-boys-don-t-cry">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3211802110" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7851855556053416938">I don’t think I’ve ever felt so warm and so anxious all in one go. Anxious because I didn’t want the story to end in a certain way yet warm because I was met with familiarities throughout the Bridgeman’s journey. Thankfully the ending suited.<br/><br/>I think overall the story was beautiful and I’m looking forward to a sequel should one be released. It raised important points throughout any young mans life and that can clearly be shown through the reflections of the ages of the different characters.<br/><br/>The on</span>
  <span id="freeText7851855556053416938" style="display:none">I don’t think I’ve ever felt so warm and so anxious all in one go. Anxious because I didn’t want the story to end in a certain way yet warm because I was met with familiarities throughout the Bridgeman’s journey. Thankfully the ending suited.<br /><br />I think overall the story was beautiful and I’m looking forward to a sequel should one be released. It raised important points throughout any young mans life and that can clearly be shown through the reflections of the ages of the different characters.<br /><br />The only thing that bugged me slightly was Blackman’s continued use of “Regarded” when describing an embrace between characters.<br /><br />Nevertheless a great read (I finished it within 24 hours of owning the book) and would recommend. I couldn’t put it down!</span>
  <a data-text-id="7851855556053416938" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2135254988" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2135254988" />
    <a title="Natalie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/6092526-natalie"><img alt="Natalie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1590598261p2/6092526.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2135254988?book_show_action=true">Sep 28, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Natalie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Natalie" href="/user/show/6092526-natalie">Natalie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="ebook 9781407078366" href="/book/show/17426717-boys-don-t-cry">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6092526-natalie?shelf=dnf">dnf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2135254988" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7844304556844349566"><a href='http://tinypic.com?ref=2rxbts3' target='_blank' rel='nofollow noopener'>
  <img src='https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1506618691ra/24032789.gif' alt='Image and video hosting by TinyPic' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
</a><br/><br/>I genuinely don't understand the high rating for this book. I'm so bored reading it that I just cannot finish. First off, I was quite unsure on whether I was up for reading <i>Boys Don't Cry</i> but it's not a long book so I thought I'd give it a go. Personally, I don't see the point of this story in the slightest.<br/><br/>So Dante's ex-girlfriend shows up at his door and leaves their baby with him then never comes back. I thought this book was going to be solely on this but it's <b>not.</b> Basically there's some</span>
  <span id="freeText7844304556844349566" style="display:none"><a href="http://tinypic.com?ref=2rxbts3" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1506618691ra/24032789.gif" alt="Image and video hosting by TinyPic" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>I genuinely don't understand the high rating for this book. I'm so bored reading it that I just cannot finish. First off, I was quite unsure on whether I was up for reading <i>Boys Don't Cry</i> but it's not a long book so I thought I'd give it a go. Personally, I don't see the point of this story in the slightest.<br><br>So Dante's ex-girlfriend shows up at his door and leaves their baby with him then never comes back. I thought this book was going to be solely on this but it's <b>not.</b> Basically there's some P.O.V chapters from his brother Adam, and I'm wondering why that's even relevant at this point that it seems out of place. I mean, at the start, there's one of Adam overhearing his dad yell at Dante about knocking up his girlfriend and there's a few lines from Adam's P.O.V like <i>'he has a kid? Oh my god'</i> kind of thing and I'm like <i>'eh, okay but why is this even relevant, who cares'</i><br><br><a href="http://tinypic.com?ref=196mvb" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">
  <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1506618691ra/24032790.gif" alt="Image and video hosting by TinyPic" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</a><br><br>As I went on, I saw that a side story was developing about Adam's sexuality (and I was about half way through before I gave up but it's still not much of a storyline at this point, just a side note. Dante giving him a hard time and asking if it's just a 'phase') but I just...don't understand why there's this new story that's emerged and the baby thing is pushed aside. Isn't the <i>'leaving the baby and Dante's hopes and dreams dies'</i> what we went into this book thinking it was about? It's weird, and almost as if the author decided to write a two books about two different issues and put them together in one book.<br><br>I'm all for reading Adam's story but it didn't help that I just found him to be really annoying and childish. Well, I don't like <i>any</i> of the characters in this book anyway, they're either irritating/being an arsehole, boring or irrelevant. I only liked the baby and that's saying something because I thought they'd annoy me the most. Dante was <i>so whiny</i> and self-absorbed, and blaming poor Emma (said baby) on literally everything when it's his fault he didn't wrap his penis in the first place. Also their dad was just a mean dad (mostly at the start).<br>And don't get me started on Dante's friends and girlfriend, jfc. They were all just douchebags, although that's probably the point, but I think Dante was a total dick to his brother so his friends didn't hate him. Like, seriously dude. AND TAKING YOUR BABY TO A BAR SO YOU DON'T MISS OUT ON DRINKS, why did he even think that was a good idea. He's stupid. How did he get four A* grades, I don't know.<br><br>Overall, this book was kind of all over the place for me. The baby story got boring very quickly, unless you want to read about a boy bathing, feeding and taking a baby out for a walk, and his brother's upbeat attitude, even after getting beaten up. It's all a little bizarre to me.<br><br>Also, I've no idea what anyone looked like. At all. (Only that the baby and Dante have black hair?) There's no character descriptions or development. I DNF'd this book so many there is more at the end but trying to read this with no idea who they are, meh.<br><br>Slightly disappointed in this one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7844304556844349566" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
